                .addSize([0, 0], [300, 250])

            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()

        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202314288')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="09572747cdd744e29539c1b093a2a94c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '41f6676d-f3a1-4571-9925-7c8dd9ce9231'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Du/Xiaomeng">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Che/Meihong" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Meihong Che</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-name">Meihong Che</p>

                     <p>Frontiers Science Center for New Organic Matter, State Key Laboratory of Advanced Chemical Power Sources, Key Laboratory of Advanced Energy Materials Chemistry (Ministry of Education), Collaborative Innovation Center of Chemical Science and Engineering (Tianjin), College of Chemistry, Nankai University, Tianjin, 300071 P.    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d104888574" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising candidate for large-scale electrochemical energy storage devices due to their low cost, abundant upstream resources, and compatible manufacturing processes with lithium-ion batteries. However, the highly active free solvent molecules in the liquid electrolyte trigger continuous interfacial side reactions between electrodes and electrolyte, which degrades the cycling performance of SIBs. Herein, a Cu-based metal-organic framework (MOF) with a uniform nanoporous channel of 1.1&nbsp;nm is exploited to confine the electrolyte. Benefiting from the highly-aggregated solvation configuration, the MOF-confined electrolyte possesses superior chemical/electrochemical and thermal stability, which guarantees its interface compatibility and flame retardancy. As a result, the batteries with the nano-confined electrolyte and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>&nbsp;cathode show an ultra-long lifetime of 3000 cycles with 93% capacity retention and decent high-temperature performance (600 cycles with 90% capacity retention). This work presents a viable method for fabricating sustainable SIBs and also provides guidance for solving the side reactions between electrolytes and electrodes in electrochemical energy storage systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314288-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have shown great potential as next-generation electrochemical energy-storage devices owing to their advantages of non-resource constrain and manufacturing process compatibility with mature lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0001_R_d104888562e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Energy Mater. Battery Energy Joule Battery Energy eScience">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, SIBs are plagued by unstable electrode–electrolyte interphase (EEI) and safety hazard, which are attributed to highly active and flammable organic electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0002_R_d104888562e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. eScience">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the desolvation process, solvents and/or anions in the electrolyte are apt to react with a high-oxidation-state cathode and low-reduction-state anode, and their decomposition leads to the degeneration of EEI (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0001">1a</a>). As the cycle progresses, this detrimental event continues to occur, which causes the battery capacity attenuation. Moreover, the most frequently used carbonate ester- and ether-based electrolytes are flammable and explosive, resulting in severe security concerns. Therefore, reducing the interfacial side reactions and enhancing the thermal stability of the electrolyte are critical to building long-life and high-safety SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0003_R_d104888562e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e4a6ac43-24e2-4a5f-a428-3286523dda92/adfm202314288-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e4a6ac43-24e2-4a5f-a428-3286523dda92/adfm202314288-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e95363bc-e1e4-485c-bee3-8ac06c2f9c7c/adfm202314288-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e4a6ac43-24e2-4a5f-a428-3286523dda92/adfm202314288-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparison of the liquid electrolyte and the MOF-confined electrolyte in batteries. a) Interfacial issues between liquid electrolytes and electrodes; b) Advantages and structure of MOF-confined electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To address the above issues, formidable efforts have been devoted to exploring advanced electrolyte systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0004_R_d104888562e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Energy Adv. Sci. Nat. Energy SmartMat">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the high-concentration electrolytes (HCEs) and localized high-concentration electrolytes (LHCEs) attract massive attention because their highly aggregative solvation configuration (aggregates (AGGs)) can facilitate the construction of stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI), thus reducing the detrimental interfacial side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0005_R_d104888562e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett. Nano Energy Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, such liquid-based electrolytes are still hindered by the safety problem due to the leakage and flammability of organic solvents, especially at high temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0006_R_d104888562e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, although the safety issues can be effectively solved by using emerging solid-state electrolytes, the inferior interfacial contraction severely restricts their practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0007_R_d104888562e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Joule Chem. Sci. Nano Energy Mater. Today Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing new strategies to simultaneously achieve high safety and high EEI stability still remains challenging for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) are a class of porous crystalline materials composed of metal ions (or clusters) and organic ligands.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0008_R_d104888562e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Science Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Chin. Chem. Lett.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Given their permanent nano/sub-nano pore size, MOFs would be the promising host to physically confine and chemically bond with the liquid electrolyte, promising stable EEI and thermal stabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0009_R_d104888562e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. J. Phys. Chem. Chem. Eur. J.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various MOF materials, Cu-MOF-74 possesses the highest density of open metal sites (OMS), which is the key to enabling the electrolyte to realize high safety and stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0001">1b</a>). Thanks to the unique structure of MOF, the electrolyte inside the pores is divided into physically confined parts and chemically coordinated ones. During battery operation, the physically confined portion can migrate to the interface under the force of an electric field, enabling good interfacial contact between the electrolyte and electrodes. Thus, introducing this MOF to the SIB system would be worth trying.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we first utilize the Cu-MOF-74 as the confinement skeleton of the SIB electrolyte, which provides nanochannels to physically confine the solvents and creates OMS to chemically coordinate with Na salts. Through the combination of DFT simulation and solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (SS-NMR), it is revealed that the confinement effect induces the electrolyte to form a highly aggregative solvated structure, which facilitates to suppress the EEI side reactions and improves the thermal stability of the electrolyte. Consequently, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode with the MOF-confined electrolyte achieves an ultra-long lifetime of 3000 cycles with 93% capacity retention. Moreover, the battery with nano-confined electrolyte can operate at a high operating temperature of 60 °C for over 600 cycles. The nano-confined strategy sheds light on the development of electrolyte materials for sustainable batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314288-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314288-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation of the Nano-Confined Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>The Cu-MOF-74, a typical porous material composed of Cu<sup>2+</sup> ion nodes and 2, 5-dihydroxyterephthalic acid ligands, was employed as a host material to confine the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0010_R_d104888562e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2a</a>, the residual <i>N</i>, <i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF) solvents from the preparation process coordinated with Cu<sup>2+</sup> centers in the pristine MOF (i.e., Cu-MOF-74 with DMF), and they were easily removed to form actived-Cu-MOF-74 via solvent exchanging and vacuum drying, which helped to exposure abundant OMS. Meanwhile, these unsaturated metal sites were easily coordinated with the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/50142b90-831a-4aa7-8fff-fe8bcedfe197/adfm202314288-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>ClO</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>−</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{ClO}}_4^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> from the loaded NaClO<sub>4</sub> in the Cu-MOF-74-confined electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0010_R_d104888562e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After removing the guest molecules coordinated with the OMS of MOFs through solvent exchanging and vacuum heating, the coordinating configuration of copper metal sites shifted from a six-coordinate octahedral geometry to a five-coordinate square pyramid. Upon confining the electrolyte into the pores of MOFs, the anions were spontaneously coordinated with the OMS, accelerating the dissociation of sodium salts (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2b</a>). The preparation process of the MCE is schematically illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8250b629-c83d-4647-80ba-7795af3ce977/adfm202314288-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8250b629-c83d-4647-80ba-7795af3ce977/adfm202314288-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bca2b86a-d697-4e71-a54d-0ba16287193a/adfm202314288-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8250b629-c83d-4647-80ba-7795af3ce977/adfm202314288-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Evolution coordination environment of Cu<sup>2+</sup> open metal sites during liquid electrolyte loading. b) Schematic illustration of the open metal site under different conditions. c) XRD patterns of Cu-MOF-74 under different conditions. d) BET curves of the activated MOF and MOF-confined electrolyte. e) TGA curves of the MOF-confined electrolyte and bulk liquid electrolyte. Digital photo (f) and SEM images (g) of surface (i) and cross-section (ii) of the MOF-confined electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The evolution of the coordination environment inside Cu-MOF-74 was further confirmed through powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2c</a>). After removing the residual DMF, the (4, −2, 0) peak of the activated sample almost disappeared, which agreed with the previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0011_R_d104888562e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. Sci. Science">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, this peak of the Cu-MOF-74 confined electrolyte (MCE) reappeared, demonstrating that the unsaturated metal sites were occupied by <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6b3551a7-2994-449e-bb39-434aca90a715/adfm202314288-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>ClO</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>−</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{ClO}}_4^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>. Also, the significantly decreased surface area validated that the liquid electrolyte was successfully confined inside the MOF channels (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2d</a>). Benefiting from the confinement effect of the Cu-MOF-74, the MCE possessed remarkably improved thermal stability compared with bulk liquid electrolyte (BLE) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2e</a>), which made it possible to operate batteries under high-temperature conditions.</p>
            
            <p>To handily use the MCE, the MOF powder was fabricated into a freestanding film with a large scale of 20 × 6 cm<sup>2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2f</a>), and then the electrolyte was loaded. According to the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images, the resultant MCE film was composed of firmly and closely packed Cu-MOF-74 particles and fibrous PTFE, and its thickness is about 46&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0002">2g</a>). Although PTFE is known to be unstable with the Na anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the reaction product NaF is beneficial for forming robust SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0012_R_d104888562e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The energy-dispersive spectrometer (EDS) elemental mappings revealed that C, O, Na, Cu, Cl, and F were uniformly distributed in the MCE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), which indicated that the NaClO<sub>4</sub>-PC electrolyte was evenly dispersed in the MCE. Interestingly, the MCE film owned the flexible freestanding feature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The electrolyte content was investigated via mass loading measurements, and only trace amounts of liquid electrolyte (&lt;1.6&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, less than 1.34&nbsp;µL cm<sup>−2</sup>) were encapsulated into the nano-channels of the MOF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the inexpensive and readily available raw materials, as well as the simple synthesis process, this MOF membrane does not significantly increase manufacturing costs compared to commercial Celgard separators (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0013_R_d104888562e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314288-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Structure of the Lean Electrolyte Under Nano-Channels</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The interaction between MOF and NaClO<sub>4</sub> was investigated through Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectrometer, Raman, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and SS-NMR. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the FT-IR spectra of pristine Cu-MOF-74 powder and MCE, and some new peaks emerged at 626, 870, and 950 cm<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0010_R_d104888562e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> after the electrolyte was loaded into the MOF, which were assigned to the vibration between Cu<sup>2+</sup> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d2afe8ca-7ac1-4243-a18c-458c78be59cd/adfm202314288-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>ClO</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>−</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{ClO}}_4^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0014_R_d104888562e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3b</a> exhibits the Raman spectra of the propylene carbonate (PC) solvent, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub>-PC electrolyte, 3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub>-PC electrolyte, and the MCE, and the peak gradually shifted to a high Raman shift as the Na salt concentration increased. To accurately determine the solvation structure of the liquid electrolyte inside MOF channels, we applied Raman peak deconvolution to calculate the coordination number (CN) values by integrating the peak areas of the free and coordinated solvents (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). Since we assumed that both free and coordinated solvents have similar Raman absorption sensitivities, the ratio of peak areas between the free and coordinated solvents should be proportional to their respective quantities. According to the same method for CN of LIBs electrolyte,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0015_R_d104888562e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we can estimate the CNs of each solvent to infer the quantities of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2f6ce4fe-da4f-4969-a231-214ed2f610fc/adfm202314288-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>ClO</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>−</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{ClO}}_4^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> anions participating in the solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup> using the following equation.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202314288-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aa35f8c0-3600-4ce9-ae4b-e28347098fb3/adfm202314288-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0005.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>A</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                                       <mo>−</mo>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msub>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>A</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>A</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>N</mi>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>C</mi>
                                    <mi>Na</mi>
                                 </msub>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>C</mi>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                 </msub>
                              </mfrac>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\frac{{{{A}_{{\mathrm{C}} - {\mathrm{S}}}}}}{{{{A}_{{\mathrm{C}} - {\mathrm{S}}}} + {{A}_{{\mathrm{F}} - {\mathrm{S}}}}}} = N\frac{{{{C}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}}}}{{{{C}_{\mathrm{S}}}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/847b9c96-8881-48da-b4a6-c83c58a52fea/adfm202314288-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/847b9c96-8881-48da-b4a6-c83c58a52fea/adfm202314288-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/11355d72-b26b-4e51-8bab-74205c102e80/adfm202314288-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/847b9c96-8881-48da-b4a6-c83c58a52fea/adfm202314288-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FT-IR spectra of pristine Cu-MOF-74 powder and MOF-confined electrolyte. b) Raman spectra of PC solvent, diluted PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>), saturated PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub> (3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>), and MOF-confined electrolyte. c) Solid state <sup>23</sup>Na MAS-NMR spectra of pristine NaClO<sub>4</sub> salts and MOF-confined electrolyte. Two possible Na<sup>+</sup> transport paths: d) hopping with the O sites, e) anion-reinforced migration (color code: Cu, blue; Na, green; O, red; Cl, yellow). f) Energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> migration in the two paths.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In this equation, <i>A</i><sub>C−S</sub> and <i>A</i><sub>F−S</sub> represent the Raman peak areas of the coordinated solvents and free solvents, respectively. <i>C</i><sub>Na</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>S</sub> are the concentrations for NaClO<sub>4</sub> and PC solvents, respectively. <i>N</i> is the CN value of solvents. Through this method, we are able to determine the CN values of the PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub> liquid electrolyte at different concentrations ranging from dilute to saturate. There is a linear relationship (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information) between the peak area ratio (the solvated and the free solvent) and the mole ratio (PC solvent and NaClO<sub>4</sub> salts). Therefore, based on the peak areal ratio of free solvent and solvated solvent inside the MOF, we can calculate the MR (mole ratio) value (about 1:2.6) of Na<sup>+</sup>/PC in the electrolyte within the MOF pores. Based on the above discussion, we can calculate that the electrolyte concentration within the MOF pores is ≈4.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> with a CN value of 1.42. As a result, the electrolyte within the MOF pores is found to exhibit a highly aggregated state, with a higher degree of aggregation than the saturated electrolyte. We also performed a simple optimization of the solvation structure of the bulk electrolyte and the MOF-confined electrolyte, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information, suggesting that its configuration was even more aggregated than the saturated state. The MCE minimized the amount of free solvent, which dramatically reduced the decomposition of solvent molecules. Therefore, this MOF-confinement strategy can effectively prevent solvent molecules from undergoing side reactions at the interface.</p>
            
            <p><sup>23</sup>Na SS-NMR was employed to further reveal the exact solvation configuration and exact ratio of the two type electrolyte inside the MCE. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3c</a>, the MCE exhibited two distinct peaks: the one at −58.5&nbsp;ppm agreed well with the NaClO<sub>4</sub> salts that belonged to the physically confined electrolyte (named Na-1), and the other peak at −40.6&nbsp;ppm was ascribed to the electrolytes chemically bonded with the OMS (named as Na-2).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0016_R_d104888562e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ratio of the Na-1 to Na-2 was calculated to be 2.88:2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, we also conducted SS-NMR on the cycled MCE (after 3000 cycles). It was observed that the signals of the two types of sodium ions were clearly presented. However, their ratio had changed to 1:2.4, indicating that a portion of the electrolyte had been consumed during the electrochemical reaction. Based on previous literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0009_R_d104888562e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. J. Phys. Chem. Chem. Eur. J.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the change in this ratio is caused by the migration of a portion of the electrolyte from the physically confined region to the interface, where it maintains ion conduction and is subsequently consumed during ongoing electrochemical processes. The NVP//MCE//Na cell was assembled without any liquid electrolyte added. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information, there were apparent liquid electrolyte-related FTIR peaks that can be detected inside the different depths of the cycled NVP cathode after 500 cycles.</p>
            
            <p>In addition, the DFT calculation was performed to analyze the migration path of the two types of Na<sup>+</sup> ions inside the MOF channels, and the optimized positions of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the MOF channels were presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information. The Na-1 ions hopped between the O hollow nodes on the MOF matrix (Path 1, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3d</a>), while the Na-2 ions migrated along the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/652db808-6cc1-4fc9-95b0-a9d1408d6fa2/adfm202314288-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>ClO</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>−</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{ClO}}_4^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> ions coordinated with OMS (Path 2, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3e</a>). Interestingly, the anion-induced path had a lower migration energy barrier (0.36&nbsp;eV) than that of the O site-based path (0.82&nbsp;eV), suggesting that the anion induced the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transfer path (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0003">3f</a>). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information, both of these migration pathways contribute to the ion conductivity of the battery during its operation. More than 0.5&nbsp;eV difference in the activation energy barrier between the two types of electrolyte is ascribed to the ordered arrangement of anions in the pore, which are coordinated with OMS, providing a shorter migration path for sodium ions and promoting their movement through a Grotthuss-like mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0010_R_d104888562e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0014_R_d104888562e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0017_R_d104888562e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, the chemical coordination between anions and metal nodes effectively enhances the dissociation of sodium ions and reduces the energy barrier for their migration.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314288-sec-0050-title">2.3 Properties of the MOF-Confined Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>Based on the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests of the symmetric cells (SS//MCE//SS) (stainless steel), the ion conductivity of the as-prepared MCE was calculated to be 2.1 × 10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> according to the Formula&nbsp;2 in Experimental Section (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4a</a>), which was on par with various reported non-fluid electrolytes at both room and high temperatures (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of MCE for sodium ion conduction was obtained via temperature-changed EIS measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4b</a>), and it was determined to be 0.1215&nbsp;eV based on Formula&nbsp;3 in Experimental Section, which was consistent with the previously reported non-fluid electrolytes. To investigate the compatibility of the MCE with sodium metal, the Na//Na symmetric cells with the as-prepared MCEs were assembled. It was able to operate stably with a low over-potential of 220&nbsp;mV over 350 h at a current density of 0.25&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 25 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4c</a>). In contrast, the symmetric cell based on the BLE shows an obvious short circuit occurring at around 85 h, which could be attributed to the formation of sodium dendrites. The linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) was employed to detect the electrochemical stability window (ESW) of the MCE, and it was expended to 4.8&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4d</a>), which reached the top level among previously reported QSSEs for SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, according to Formula&nbsp;4 in the Experimental Section, the Na-ion transfer number (<i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub>) of the MCE was calculated to be 0.62 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4e</a>), which is much higher than that of liquid electrolyte (0.28) and is comparable to other reported non-fluid electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ac32e71f-0ec2-4b02-bcd9-c9bec46eafdd/adfm202314288-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ac32e71f-0ec2-4b02-bcd9-c9bec46eafdd/adfm202314288-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9f507b26-c9e6-4c20-ae7a-eee1b2c05b5a/adfm202314288-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ac32e71f-0ec2-4b02-bcd9-c9bec46eafdd/adfm202314288-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ionic conductivities and corresponding impedance of the symmetric coin cells (SS//MCE//SS) at room temperature. b) The corresponding activation energies of the MCE and BLE. c) Electrochemical performance of Na//Na symmetrical cells with the MCE and BLE. d) Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) curve of the MCE. e) Polarization curves and the electrochemical impedance spectra (inset) of the MCEs. f) Photographs of flammability tests of the as-prepared MCE and LE infiltrated in the glass fibers.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A flammability test was applied to confirm the flame retardancy of the MCE. Obviously, the typical liquid electrolyte (simply dropped on glass fiber, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0004">4f</a>) was completely burned under igniting for only 1 s. In contrast, the MCE was not burned even after continuously contacting the fire for 5 s, suggesting its high thermal stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314288-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performance of Half and Full Cells with the MOF-Confined Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>NVP//Na cells based on the MCE were fabricated and tested at both room temperature (25 °C) and high temperature (60 °C) to evaluate their electrochemical properties. The NVP owned a 3D NASICON structure and uniform spherical morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5a</a>, the NVP//Na half-cell, coupled with the MCE (red curve), demonstrated ultra-long cycling stability (3000 cycles) at 2 C (1 C = 117&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) with an average CE of 99.95%. In addition, the NVP//MCE//Na cell exhibited an ultra-high capacity retention of 93%, which was significantly superior to that of most reported non-fluid electrolyte-based SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5b</a>). Moreover, the PXRD pattern of the MCE was almost unchanged even after a long-term cycling process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), which suggested its excellent electrochemical stability. In addition, the (4, −2, 0) peak of the liquid electrolyte and the Cu metal observed in the XRD patterns still existed in the MCE film obtained from the NVP//MCE//Na configuration even after 500 and 3000 cycles, indicating that the nanoconfinement effect always exists within the MCE. Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information, two different types of liquid electrolytes exist in the channels of the MOF: the physically confined electrolyte (type 1) and the chemically confined one (type 2). The type 1 electrolyte is beneficial for wetting the cathode during cycling, as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a,b</a>, Supporting Information. Moreover, the type 2 electrolyte can be retained within the MOF channels even after 3000 cycles, as confirmed by the XRD results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The consumption of the liquid electrolyte can only be attributed to the physically confined liquid electrolyte. Additionally, the nano-confinement persists throughout the electrochemical cycling process as both physically and chemically confined liquid electrolytes remain within the MOF channels (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2fb37c3b-39e7-4de5-9a49-e82952cc0fce/adfm202314288-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2fb37c3b-39e7-4de5-9a49-e82952cc0fce/adfm202314288-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/624fe20a-e510-4cd4-916b-fa6cba1b1cfb/adfm202314288-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2fb37c3b-39e7-4de5-9a49-e82952cc0fce/adfm202314288-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Long-term cycling performance of the NVP//MCE//Na and NVP//BLE//Na cells (2 C at room temperature). b) Capacity retention of the NVP//Na&nbsp;batteries with the non-fluid electrolytes of the published papers. c) Selected discharge-charge curves of the NVP//MCE//Na cell at 2 C rate. d) High-temperature (60 °C) cycling stability of the MCE and BLE with NVP//Na cells at 1 C rate and its selected discharge/charge curves (e). f) Selected discharge–discharge curves of the MCE coupled with the high voltage cathode of NVPF. g) Charge–discharge profiles of the NTP//NVP full cell at various current rates. h) Cycling performance of the NTP//NVP&nbsp;full cell.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To the best of our knowledge, NVP cathodes with the MCE displayed the highest capacity retention and the best cycling performance among the as-reported Na-based non-fluid electrolytes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the charge–discharge curves of the NVP//Na cell at different cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5c</a>) substantiated its exceptional cycling performance. However, the NVP//Na cell with BLE showed continuous capacity decay under the same test conditions. Subsequently, the rate performance of MCE-based cells was measured at different current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C, and the discharge capacities were 107, 104, 100, 87, and 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). This superior structural stability was also confirmed by the PXRD pattern of the cycled NVP cathode pellet (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). To investigate the differences between the MOF-constrained strategy and the traditional dilute solution strategy, the NVP//Na cells with different volumes of liquid electrolyte were assembled. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information, a gradual decrease appears in the cycling stability and Coulombic efficiency of the batteries by reducing the amount of electrolyte from 40 to 4&nbsp;µL. This is mainly due to the formation of a thicker and less dense interfacial layer derived from the interfacial side reactions between the liquid electrolyte and the electrode when using the Celgard separators. The above results testify to the outstanding nano-confinement effect of MOF-confined electrolytes.</p>
            
            <p>The high-temperature electrochemical performance of the MCE was also investigated, and the MCE-based NVP//Na cell demonstrated an impressive capacity retention of nearly 93% after 600 cycles at 1 C under 60 °C, which was better than that of the BLE-based cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5d</a>). Based on our current understanding, the high-temperature performance of NVP//MCE//Na cell is at an exceptionally high level (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The charge–discharge curves of the NVP cathode coupled with the MCE electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5e</a>) revealed its good stability under high temperatures. Particularly, the MCE-based NVP//Na cell exhibited a high cycling stability even with a high cathode mass loading of 8.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). It achieved a high initial capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity was maintained at 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 600 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of 90%.</p>
            
            <p>To further investigate the high-voltage stability of the MCE, the 4.2&nbsp;V-class Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F (shorted as NVPF) was employed as a cathode material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0018_R_d104888562e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NVPF//Na half-cell presented a discharge capacity of 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with a high capacity retention of 95.4% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), which confirmed its good high-voltage stability.</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, we constructed an all-phosphate-based full cell using the NVP cathode and NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTP) anode, and the NTP possessed a desodiation voltage of 2.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5g</a>, the NVP//MCE//NTP full cell exhibited a voltage plateau at ≈1.25&nbsp;V in the first cycle, delivering discharge capacities of 105.2, 102.5, and 87.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.25, 0.5, and 0.1 C, respectively. After 100 cycles, the NVP//NTP&nbsp;full cell demonstrated capacity retention of 70.3% with a Coulombic efficiency of 99.5% at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0005">5h</a>), indicating its good interface compatibility. Moreover, the use of the MCE membrane does not reduce the volumetric energy density through the NVP//HC fullcell testing (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0019_R_d104888562e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314288-sec-0070-title">2.5 Compatibility of the MOF-Confined Electrolyte with the Electrodes</h3>
            
            <p>SEM and transmission electron microscope (TEM) were employed to observe the morphology of the cycled NVP cathode. After 200 cycles, the NVP cathode cycled within the BLE exhibited several cracks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a–d</a>, Supporting Information) with a thick CEI layer (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6a,b</a>). In sharp contrast, the NVP cathode cycled within the MCE exhibited a smooth morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24e–h</a>, Supporting Information) with an ultrathin and uniform CEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6c,d</a>). As schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6e</a>, the global structure of the NVP cathode using BLE electrolyte was destroyed with the formation of various cracks and thick CEI layers due to the serious solvent-induced decomposition and the co-intercalation of solvated Na-ions. However, when the lean liquid MCE electrolyte with high aggregated configurations was used, the NVP cathode exhibited a crack-free structure and ultra-thin CEI layer, which suggested the successful reduction of interfacial side reactions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314288-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bee77b1c-d39d-4325-aee0-492abf074320/adfm202314288-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bee77b1c-d39d-4325-aee0-492abf074320/adfm202314288-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8721d441-d4b5-42e5-b164-a51dae4e7990/adfm202314288-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bee77b1c-d39d-4325-aee0-492abf074320/adfm202314288-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314288-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of the cycled NVP cathode using the BLE and the MCE. SEM and TEM images of the cycled NVP cathode (after 200 cycles) harvested from the NVP//Na half cell with the BLE (a,b) and the MCE (c,d). Schematic diagram of the CEI formation mechanism of the cells with the BLE and the MCE (e). Argon-etching XPS C 1s (f,g) and Raman spectra of the cathode of the BLE (h) and the MCE (i) under different depths.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further verify our conjecture shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6e</a>, argon-etching XPS tests were employed at three different durations (0, 18, and 36 s) to collect more detailed information on the CEI layer on the cycled NVP cathode. For the C 1s XPS spectra of the two samples, the BLE-based sample showed an obvious HO─C═O (288.2&nbsp;eV) peak which was attributed to the decomposition of PC solvent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6f</a>), while there was no signal of such peak for the MCE-based samples from surface to bulk (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0005_R_d104888562e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett. Nano Energy Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the large amount of PC solvent from the BLE constantly reacted with NVP particles to form a thick CEI, while the side reactions between the free solvent/salts and NVP cathode were significantly reduced due to the “nano-confinement” effect. Moreover, the content of sodium and chlorine elements in the CEI of the BLE-based cell was significantly higher than those of the MCE-based cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). This finding suggested that fewer sodium salts were involved in the formation of the CEI in the MCE-based cathode. The commercial NVP cathode particles are often coated with a thin carbon layer to enhance their electronic conduction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0020_R_d104888562e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So, the carbon layer on the NVP could be indirectly used as an indicator of the depth of the CEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0021_R_d104888562e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - eScience">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Raman spectroscopy with different depths (from 0 to 50&nbsp;µm) was employed to further study the CEI layer of NVP cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314288-fig-0006">6h,i</a>). The NVP cathode with MCE exhibits a clear D band and G band, which is similar to the pristine NVP sample, while the BLE-based sample shows minimal carbon signals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0022_R_d104888562e1247" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It reveals that the CEI layer of the MCE-based cell is much thinner than that of the MCE-based cell, which agrees well with the etching-XPS and TEM results.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the enhancement of the anode side was observed by SEM. The cycled sodium metal harvested from the Na//BLE//Na half-cell showed a rough surface morphology with numerous dendrites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26a,b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the cycled sodium anode harvested from the Na//MCE//Na half-cell displayed a smooth surface morphology without dendritic sodium (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26c,d</a>, Supporting Information). The XPS spectra of cycled Na anodes are demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information. Specifically, as shown in the C 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a</a>, Supporting Information), four prominent peaks centering around 284.7, 286.5, 288.5, and 289.8&nbsp;eV can be assigned to C─C, C─O, C═O, and C─F species, respectively, indicative of the simultaneous participation of FEC and PC in the SEI formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0005_R_d104888562e1267" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett. Nano Energy Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the O 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27b</a>, Supporting Information), the two peaks around 533.2 and 531.7&nbsp;eV can be attributed to the C─O and C═O, which belong to the byproducts of the PC solvent. In addition, the peak of 531.2&nbsp;eV was from the decomposition of the NaClO<sub>4</sub> salts. For the F 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27c</a>, Supporting Information), the two peaks around 687.6 and 684.5&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to C─F<sub>3</sub> and Na─F, which come from the sodium and organic solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0023_R_d104888562e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the degradation products on the Na anode surface of these two electrolytes have similar chemical compositions. However, the SEI of the MCE showed superior enrichments in inorganic species of Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na-F than the BLE samples, which is generated from the less decomposition and interfacial reaction of the organic solvent in the MCE systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0024_R_d104888562e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In brief, the MCE has successfully served the purpose of depleting free/weakly coordinated solvent molecules within the solvation sheaths of solvated sodium ions and inhibiting the undesirable side reaction between Na anode and solvent molecules. These results verified that the CEI and SEI were significantly enhanced due to the distinctive aggregated electrolyte configuration of the MCE. In addition, the cross-sectional SEM images and EDS maps of the NVP//MCE//Na cell after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), and very tight MCE/Na interfaces are observed. This result clearly confirms that the MCE film tightly contacts with Na metal anode, which greatly reduces their interfacial resistance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314288-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we constructed a novel Cu-MOF-74-confined electrolyte with an excellent interface and thermal stability for SIBs. The MCE with a highly aggregated configuration effectively reduced the interfacial side reactions and lowered the flammability, enhancing the cycling and high-temperature performance of the SIBs. Moreover, the unique chemical coordination between the OMS and anion built a rapid anion-reinforced migration pathway for sodium ions, which guaranteed a high Na<sup>+</sup> transfer number. As a result, NVP//Na cells with the MCE achieved an ultra-long lifetime of 3000 cycles at 2 C and exhibited outstanding high-temperature performance (93% capacity retention after 600 cycles at 60 °C). The nano-confined strategy opens an avenue for constructing safe and stable electrolyte materials for energy storage and conversion.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314288-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0120-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the solvents employed in this paper were purchased from DAMAO Chemical Regent Industries without further purification. The other materials were listed as follows: Polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) aqueous solution (10&nbsp;wt%, Aladdin), Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O (99.5%, Aladdin), 2, 5-dihydroxyterephthalic acid (99.5%, J&amp;K Scientific), Glass fiber membrane (GF/A, Whatman), NVP (Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.), NVPF (Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.), NTP (Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.), Super P (HF KeJing Co., Ltd.), polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, HF KeJing Co., Ltd.), NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Suzhou DoDochem Co., Ltd.), PC (Suzhou DoDochem Co., Ltd.), and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC, Suzhou DoDochem Co., Ltd.).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0130-title"> Preparation of Cu-MOF-74</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Cu-MOF-74 was synthesized according to the previously reported hydrothermal method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314288-bib-0010_R_d104888562e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a specific process, the Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O (0.996&nbsp;g, 4&nbsp;mmol) and 2,5-dihydroxyterephthalic acid (0.396&nbsp;g, 2&nbsp;mmol) were dissolved into a mixture solvent of DMF and ethanol in a ratio of 20:1. Then the precursor solution was transferred into an oven of 80 °C for 24&nbsp;h to form Cu-MOF-74. The residual solvent molecules in the pores of Cu-MOF-74 could be removed through solvent exchange with anhydrous methanol. Afterward, the sample was activated under vacuum at 150 °C for 12 h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0140-title"> Preparation of MOF Film</h4>
                  
                  <p>The MOF-based slurry was obtained by mixing 90&nbsp;wt% activated MOF sample with 10&nbsp;wt% PTFE aqueous solution. Then a few drops of ethanol were added and quickly stirred to mix the sample evenly for 15&nbsp;min till it formed sticky MOF putty. The as-obtained MOF putty was then uniformly spread on the clean panel to be rolled into a flexible film. To reduce the inter-particle pores, the MOF film was compacted under a pressure of 10 T. The film was cut into small circular plates (<i>Φ</i> = 16&nbsp;mm) and was dried overnight under vacuum in an oven at 150 °C to remove the residual water in the pores. Afterward, it was transferred into a glove box for further use.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0150-title"> Preparation of Carbonate Electrolytes</h4>
                  
                  <p>Prior to the process, the carbonate electrolytes (PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub>) with 5% FEC were prepared (the FEC is one of the best-known and most studied additives for passivating sodium anode). Typically, 1.22&nbsp;g of NaClO<sub>4</sub> salts and 0.69&nbsp;g of FEC were mixed with 10&nbsp;mL of PC solvent, and the mixture was stirred at 50 °C for 5&nbsp;h. The other concentrations (0.5, 2, and 3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) were prepared using the same method by adjusting the salt amount.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0160-title"> Preparation of the MOF-Confined Electrolyte</h4>
                  
                  <p>The as-prepared Cu-MOF-74 plate was immersed in a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte at 90 °C for 48 h to load PC electrolyte molecules into the MOF channels. After that, the plate was removed from the liquid electrolyte, and its surface was carefully wiped with tissues. The plates were then dried at 70 °C for 4 h, followed by a vacuum process at room temperature for 24 h to remove the excessive solvents.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0170-title"> Electrode Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The cathode electrode was prepared by casting a slurry consisting of NVP, super P, and PVDF with a weight ratio of 8:1:1 onto an aluminum foil, and the mixture was dried at 110 °C for 12 h. The mass loading of the NVP cathode materials was ≈2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The NVP cathode was then cut into electrode plates with a diameter of 10&nbsp;mm. The high-voltage NVPF cathode, NTP anode, and hard carbon anode were prepared by the same method. The high-loading cathode was achieved by adjusting the distance between the doctor-blade and the substrate.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314288-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314288-sec-0180-title"> Cell Assembly and Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the CR2032 coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.01&nbsp;ppm). The MCE-based cells were assembled without the addition of extra liquid electrolyte. For comparison, cells utilizing the same electrodes and 60&nbsp;µL of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> PC-NaClO<sub>4</sub> with 5% FEC electrolyte were also assembled. The glass fiber was used as the separator, and all cells were kept on an open circuit for 8 h before the electrochemical tests. The MCE-based full cell was assembled using the NVP anode and NTP cathode, with an N/P ratio of 1.2:1. Galvanostatic electrochemical measurements were conducted using the LAND battery tester system at room temperature (25 °C) and high temperature (60 °C).</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The ionic conductivity of the MCEs was measured using a simple stainless steel (SS)//MCE//SS configuration, employing EIS in the frequency range of 0.1–10<sup>6</sup> Hz and at a 0.01&nbsp;V amplitude. The measurements were conducted across a temperature range of 30 to 70 °C. The specific ionic conductivity value (<i>σ</i>) was calculated according to Formula&nbsp;2

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202314288-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/dd4e5ed1-7468-4c7f-bb61-1f92b86b61cd/adfm202314288-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314288:adfm202314288-math-0007" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202314288-math-0007.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>σ</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mi>L</mi>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mi>R</mi>
                                    <mi>S</mi>
                                 </mrow>
